The next day, the carrier USS Saipan delivered 25 war-weary ground attack Ex-USMC AU-1 Corsairs (flown by VMA-212 at the end of the Korean War). During three months operating over Dien Bien Phu and Viêt-Nam, the Corsairs flew 959 combat sorties totaling 1,335 flight hours. They dropped some 700 tons of bombs and fired more than 300 rockets and 70.000 20mm rounds. Six aircraft were damaged and two shot down by Viet Minh.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In September 1954, F4U-7 Corsairs were loaded aboard the Dixmude and brought back to France in November. The surviving Ex-USMC AU-1s were taken to the Philippines and returned to the U.S. Navy. In 1956, Flotille 15F returned to South Vietnam, equipped with F4U-7 Corsairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suez_Crisis">Suez Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Suez Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The 14.F and 15.F Flotillas also took part in the Anglo-French-Israeli seizure of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in October 1956, code-named <a href="/wiki/Operation_Musketeer_(1956)" title="Operation Musketeer (1956)">Operation Musketeer</a>. The Corsairs were painted with yellow and black recognition stripes for this operation. They were tasked with destroying Egyptian Navy ships at Alexandria but the presence of U.S. Navy ships prevented the successful completion of the mission. On 3 November, 16 F4U-7s attacked airfields in the Delta, with one corsair shot down by anti-aircraft fire. Two more Corsairs were damaged when landing back on the carriers. The Corsairs engaged in Operation Musketeer dropped a total of 25 tons of bombs, fired more than 500 rockets and 16,000 20mm rounds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algerian_War">Algerian War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Algerian War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As soon as they disembarked from the carriers that took part in Operation Musketeer, at the end of 1956, all three Corsair Flotillas, moved to Telergma and Oran airfields in Algeria from where they provided CAS and helicopter escort. They were joined by the new "<a href="/wiki/Flottille_17F" title="Flottille 17F">Flottille 17F</a>", established at Hyères in April 1958.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>French F4U-7 Corsairs (with some loaned AU-1s) of the 12F, 14F, 15F and 17F Flotillas conducted missions during the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_War" title="Algerian War">Algerian War</a> between 1955 and 1962. Between February and March 1958, several strikes and CAS missions were launched from the "<a href="/wiki/USS_Belleau_Wood_(CVL-24)" title="USS Belleau Wood (CVL-24)">Bois-Belleau</a>", the only carrier involved in the Algeria War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Les_Corsair_fran.C3.A7ais_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Les_Corsair_fran.C3.A7ais-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U-Corsair.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="1024" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/F4U-Corsair.JPG/220px-F4U-Corsair.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/F4U-Corsair.JPG/330px-F4U-Corsair.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/F4U-Corsair.JPG/440px-F4U-Corsair.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U-Corsair.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former Argentine F4U-5NL in Aeronavale 14.F flotilla colors in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tunisia">Tunisia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tunisia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>France recognized Tunisian independence and sovereignty in 1956 but continued to station military forces at Bizerte and planned to extend the airbase. In 1961, Tunisia asked France to evacuate the base. Tunisia imposed a blockade on the base on 17 July, hoping to force its evacuation. This resulted in a battle between militiamen and the French military which lasted three days. French paratroopers, escorted by Corsairs of the 12F and 17F Flotillas, were dropped to reinforce the base and the Aéronavale launched air strikes on Tunisian troops and vehicles between 19–21 July, carrying out more than 150 sorties. Three Corsairs were damaged by ground fire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_experiments">French experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: French experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In early 1959, the <i>Aéronavale</i> experimented with the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>-era <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nord_SS.11" title="Nord SS.11">SS.11</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wire-guided" title="Wire-guided">wire-guided</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-tank_missile" title="Anti-tank missile">anti-tank missile</a> on F4U-7 Corsairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeria_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algeria-98">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> The 12.F pilots trained for this experimental program were required to "fly" the missile at approximatively two kilometers from the target on low altitude with a joystick using the right hand while keeping track of a flare on its tail, and piloting the aircraft using the left hand;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeria_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algeria-98">[93]</a></sup> an exercise that could be very tricky in a single-seat aircraft under combat conditions. Despite reportedly effective results during the tests, this armament was not used with Corsairs during the ongoing Algerian War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeria_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Algeria-98">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Aéronavale</i> used 163 Corsairs (94 F4U-7s and 69 AU-1s), the last of them used by the <a href="/wiki/Cuers" title="Cuers">Cuers</a>-based 14.F Flotilla were out of service by September 1964,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Les_Corsair_fran.C3.A7ais_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Les_Corsair_fran.C3.A7ais-1">[1]</a></sup> with some surviving for museum display or as civilian <a href="/wiki/Warbird" title="Warbird">warbirds</a>. By the early 1960s, two new modern aircraft carriers, the Clemenceau and the Foch, had entered service with the French Navy and with them a new generation of jet-powered combat aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tillman_p._192_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tillman_p._192-82">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Football_War.22">"Football War"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title='Edit section: "Football War"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Corsairs flew their final combat missions in 1969 during the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Football_War" title="Football War">Football War</a>" between <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> and <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, in service with both air forces.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2257" data-file-width="2829" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG/220px-F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG/330px-F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG/440px-F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U_7_AND_CORSAIR_11.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lynn Garrison in F4U-7 133693 – N693M leads <a href="/wiki/LTV_A-7_Corsair_II" title="LTV A-7 Corsair II">Corsair IIs</a> of VA-147, over NAS Lemoore, California, 7 July 1967 prior to first deployment to Vietnam on USS <i>Ranger</i>. The A-7A "NE-300" is the aircraft of the Air Group Commander (CAG) of Attack Carrier Air Wing 2 (CVW-2)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The conflict was allegedly triggered, though not really caused, by a disagreement over a football (soccer) match. Captain Fernando Soto of the <a href="/wiki/Honduran_Air_Force" title="Honduran Air Force">Honduran Air Force</a> shot down three <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_of_El_Salvador" title="Air Force of El Salvador">Salvadoran Air Force</a> aircraft on 17 July 1969. In the morning he shot down a <a href="/wiki/Cavalier_Mustang" title="Cavalier Mustang">Cavalier Mustang</a>, killing the pilot. In the afternoon, he shot down two FG-1s; the pilot of the second aircraft may have bailed out, but the third exploded in the air, killing the pilot. These combats were the last ones among propeller-driven aircraft in the world and also making Soto the only pilot credited with three kills in an American continental war. El Salvador did not shoot down any Honduran aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> At the outset of the Football War, El Salvador enlisted the assistance of several American pilots with <a href="/wiki/North_American_P-51_Mustang" title="North American P-51 Mustang">P-51</a> and F4U experience. Bob Love, a Korean war ace, Chuck Lyford, Ben Hall and <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Garrison" title="Lynn Garrison">Lynn Garrison</a> are believed to have flown combat missions, but it has never been confirmed. Lynn Garrison had purchased F4U-7 133693 from the French MAAG office when he retired from French naval service in 1964. It was registered N693M and was later destroyed in a 1987 crash in San Diego, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Corsair entered service in 1942. Although designed as a carrier fighter, initial operation from carrier decks proved to be troublesome. Its low-speed handling was tricky due to the left wing stalling before the right wing. This factor, together with poor visibility over the long nose (leading to one of its nicknames, "The Hose Nose"), made landing a Corsair on a carrier a difficult task. For these reasons, most Corsairs initially went to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">Marine Corps</a> squadrons who operated off land-based runways, with some early Goodyear-built examples (designated <b>FG-1A</b>) being built with fixed wings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swanboroughp404_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Swanboroughp404-42">[40]</a></sup> The USMC aviators welcomed the Corsair with open arms as its performance was far superior to the contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brewster_Buffalo" title="Brewster Buffalo">Brewster Buffalo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grumman_F4F_Wildcat" title="Grumman F4F Wildcat">Grumman F4F-3 and -4 Wildcat</a>.</p>
<p>Moreover, the Corsair was able to outperform the primary Japanese fighter, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A6M_Zero" title="A6M Zero">A6M Zero</a>. While the Zero could outturn the F4U at low speed, the Corsair was faster and could outclimb and outdive the A6M.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Styling_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Styling-102">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>This performance advantage, combined with the ability to take severe punishment, meant a pilot could place an enemy aircraft in the killing zone of the F4U's six <a href="/wiki/.50_BMG" title=".50 BMG">.50</a> (12.7 mm) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M2_Browning_machine_gun" title="M2 Browning machine gun">M2 Browning machine guns</a> and keep him there long enough to inflict major damage. The 2,300 rounds carried by the Corsair gave just under 30 seconds of fire from each gun, which, fired in three to six-second bursts, made the F4U a devastating weapon against aircraft, ground targets, and even ships.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3406" data-file-width="4797" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg/220px-F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg/330px-F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg/440px-F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U_Coursair_Udvar_Hazy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Corsair on display at the National Air and Space Museum, Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning in 1943, the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> (FAA) also received Corsairs and flew them successfully from <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> carriers in combat with the British Pacific Fleet and in Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> These were clipped-wing Corsairs, the wingtips shortened 8 in (20 cm) to clear the lower <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">overhead</a> height of RN carriers. FAA also developed a curving landing approach to overcome the F4U's deficiencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CorsairUnderside.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CorsairUnderside.jpg/220px-CorsairUnderside.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CorsairUnderside.jpg/330px-CorsairUnderside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CorsairUnderside.jpg/440px-CorsairUnderside.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CorsairUnderside.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Underside of a Corsair</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Infantrymen nicknamed the Corsair "The Sweetheart of the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_and_Palau_Islands_campaign" title="Mariana and Palau Islands campaign">Marianas</a>" and "The Angel of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">Okinawa</a>" for its roles in these campaigns. Among Navy and Marine aviators, the aircraft was nicknamed "Ensign Eliminator" and "Bent-Wing Eliminator" because it required many more hours of flight training to master than other Navy carrier-borne aircraft. It was also called simply "U-bird" or "Bent Wing Bird".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shettle_p._107_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shettle_p._107-2">[2]</a></sup> Although Allied World War II sources frequently make the claim that the Japanese called the Corsair the "Whistling Death", one source indicates that they did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Corsair has been named the official aircraft of <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> due to its connection with <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft" title="Sikorsky Aircraft">Sikorsky Aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="1011" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg/220px-F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg/330px-F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg/440px-F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U-1_Corsair_in_flight_c1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early F4U-1 in flight.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During World War II, Corsair production expanded beyond Vought to include Brewster and Goodyear models. Allied forces flying the aircraft in World War II included FAA and RNZAF. Eventually, more than 12,500 F4Us would be built, comprising 16 separate variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mjwqqv_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-mjwqqv-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-1</b> (<b>Corsair Mk I</b> <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>): The first production version of the Corsair with the distinctive "bird cage" canopy and low seating position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[N 6]</a></sup> The differences over the XF4U-1 were as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Six .50 in (12.7 mm) Browning AN/M2 machine guns were fitted in the outer wing panels, displacing fuel tanks.</li>
<li>An enlarged 237 gal (897 l) fuel tank was fitted ahead of the cockpit, in place of the fuselage armament. The cockpit was moved back by 32 in (810 mm).</li>
<li>The fuselage was lengthened by 1 ft 5 in (0.43 m).</li>
<li>The more powerful R-2800-8 Double Wasp was fitted.</li>
<li>150 pounds (68 kg) of armor plate was fitted to the cockpit and a 1.5 in (38 mm) bullet-resistant glass screen was fitted behind the curved windscreen.</li>
<li>IFF transponder equipment was fitted.</li>
<li>Curved transparent panels were incorporated into the fuselage behind the pilot's headrest.</li>
<li>The flaps were changed from deflector type to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NACA" title="NACA">NACA</a> slotted.</li>
<li>The span of the ailerons was increased while that of the flaps was decreased.</li>
<li>One 62 gal (234 l) auxiliary fuel cell (not a self-sealing type) was installed in each wing leading edge, just outboard of the guns.</li>
</ul>
<p>A land-based version for the USMC, without the folding wing capability, was built by Goodyear under the designation <b>FG-1</b>. In Fleet Air Arm service the F4U-1 was given the service name Corsair Mk I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goebel_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goebel-108">[102]</a></sup> Vought also built a single F4U-1 two-seat trainer; the Navy showed no interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-1A</b> (<b>Corsair Mk II</b>): The designation F4U-1A does not appear in lists of Corsair Bureau Numbers and was not officially used, being applied post-war to differentiate mid-to-late production F4U-1s from the early production variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_1984.2C_p._27._35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1984.2C_p._27.-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> Mid-to-late production Corsairs incorporated a new, taller and wider clear-view canopy with only two frames — very close to what the <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_hood" title="Malcolm hood">Malcolm hood</a> did for British fighter aircraft — along with a simplified clear view windscreen; the new canopy design meant that the semi-elliptical Plexiglas rear-view dorsal turtledeck "flank" windows could be omitted. The pilot's seat was raised 7 in (180 mm) which, combined with the new canopy and a lengthened tailwheel strut, allowed the pilot better visibility over the long nose. In addition to these changes the clear view panels under the cockpit were also omitted. These Corsairs introduced a 6 in (150 mm)-long stall strip just outboard of the gun ports on the right wing leading edge and improved undercarriage oleo struts which eliminated bouncing on landing, making these the first "carrier capable" F4Us. F4U-1s supplied to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">USMC</a> lacked arrester hooks and had a pneumatic tail wheel, instead of the smaller diameter solid rubber type used for carrier operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[N 7]</a></sup> Additionally, an experimental R-2800-8W engine with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_injection_(engines)" title="Water injection (engines)">water injection</a> was fitted on one of the late F4U-1As. After satisfactory results, many F4U-1As were fitted with the new powerplant. The aircraft carried 237 gal (897 l) in the main fuel tank, located in front of the cockpit, as well as an unarmored, non-self-sealing 62 gal (235 l) fuel tank in each wing. This version of the Corsair was the first to be able to carry a drop tank under the center-section. With drop tanks fitted, the fighter had a maximum ferry range of just over 1,500 mi (2,400 km).</p>
<p>A land-based version, without the folding wing capability, was built by Goodyear as the <b>FG-1A</b>. In British service, the aircraft type was modified with "clipped" wings (8 in (200 mm) was cut off each wingtip) for use on British aircraft carriers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goebel_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goebel-108">[102]</a></sup> under the designation Corsair Mk II.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1493" data-file-width="1882" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg/220px-Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg/330px-Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg/440px-Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brewster_F3A-1_Corsair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A F3A-1 in a dive</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>F3A-1</b> (<b>Corsair Mk. III</b>): This was the designation for the <a href="/wiki/Brewster_Aeronautical_Corporation" title="Brewster Aeronautical Corporation">Brewster</a>-built F4U-1. Just over 700 were built before Brewster was forced out of business. Poor production techniques and ineffective quality control meant that these aircraft were red-lined for speed and prohibited from aerobatics after several lost their wings. This was later traced to poor quality wing fittings. None of the Brewster-built Corsairs reached front line units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-1B</b>: This was an unofficial post-war designation used to identify F4U-1s modified for <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">FAA</a> use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_1984.2C_p._27._35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1984.2C_p._27.-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-1C</b>: The prototype F4U-1C, BuNo50277, appeared in August 1943 and was based on an F4U-1. A total of 200 of this variant were built July–November 1944; all were based on the F4U-1D and were built in parallel with that variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_1984_p._28._74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1984_p._28.-74">[71]</a></sup> Intended for ground-attack as well as fighter missions, the F4U-1C was similar to the F4U-1D but its six machine guns were replaced by four 20 millimeter (0.79 in) <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">AN/M2 cannons</a> with 231 rounds of ammunition per gun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[106]</a></sup> The F4U-1C was introduced to combat during 1945, most notably in the Okinawa campaign. Aviators preferred the standard armament of six .50 in (12.7 mm) machine guns since they were already more than powerful enough to destroy most Japanese aircraft, and had more ammunition and a higher rate of fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup> The weight of the Hispano cannon and their ammunition affected the flight performance, especially its agility, but the aircraft was found to be especially potent in the ground attack role.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="662" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg/220px-FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg/330px-FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg/440px-FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FG-1D_NAN3-53.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Goodyear-built FG-1D, with the later single-piece "blown" canopy used by the F4U-1D.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>F4U-1D</b> (<b>Corsair Mk IV</b>): Built in parallel with the F4U-1C, but was introduced in April 1944. It had the new -8W water-injection engine. This change gave the aircraft up to 250 hp (190 kW) more power, which, in turn, increased performance. Speed was increased from 417 mph (671 km/h) to 425 mph (684 km/h). Due to the U.S. Navy's need for fighter-bombers, it had a payload of rockets double the -1A's, as well as twin-rack plumbing for an additional belly drop tank. These modifications necessitated the need for rocket tabs (attached to fully metal-plated underwing surfaces) and bomb pylons to be bolted onto the fighter, causing extra drag. The extra fuel carried by the two drop tanks would still allow the aircraft to fly relatively long missions despite the heavy, un-aerodynamic loads. A <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_hood" title="Malcolm hood">single piece "blown" clear-view canopy</a> was adopted as standard equipment for the -1D model, and all later F4U production aircraft. Additional production was carried out by Goodyear (<b>FG-1D</b>) and Brewster (<b>F3A-1D</b>). In Fleet Air Arm service, the latter was known as the Corsair III, and both had their wingtips clipped – 8 inches (203 mm) per wing – to allow storage in the lower hangars of British carriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goebel_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goebel-108">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-1P</b>: A rare photo reconnaissance variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qqnaio_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-qqnaio-115">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_(CV-11)_in_1944.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="857" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg/220px-F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg/330px-F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg/440px-F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_%28CV-11%29_in_1944.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U-2_VFN-101_on_USS_Intrepid_(CV-11)_in_1944.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F4U-2s aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Intrepid_(CV-11)" title="USS Intrepid (CV-11)">USS <i>Intrepid</i> (CV-11)</a>. The radome on the right outer wing is just visible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>XF4U-2</b>: Special night fighter variant, equipped with two auxiliary fuel tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-2</b>: Experimental conversion of the F4U-1 Corsair into a carrier-borne night fighter, armed with five .50 in (12.7 mm) machine guns (the outboard, right gun was deleted), and fitted with Airborne Intercept (AI) radar set in a radome placed outboard on the right wing. Since Vought was preoccupied with more important projects, only 32 were converted from existing F4U-1s by the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Aircraft_Factory" title="Naval Aircraft Factory">Naval Aircraft Factory</a> and another two by front line units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[111]</a></sup> The type saw combat with VF(N)-101 aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CV-6)" title="USS Enterprise (CV-6)">USS <i>Enterprise</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Intrepid_(CV-11)" title="USS Intrepid (CV-11)">USS <i>Intrepid</i></a> in early 1944, VF(N)-75 in the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=VMF(N)-532&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="VMF(N)-532 (page does not exist)">VMF(N)-532</a> on <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tarawa" title="Battle of Tarawa">Tarawa</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1708" data-file-width="1505" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg/220px-XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg/330px-XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg/440px-XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:XF4U-3_NAN6_46.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An XF4U-3 in 1946.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>XF4U-3</b>: Experimental aircraft built to hold different engines in order to test the Corsair's performance with a variety of power plants. This variant never entered service. Goodyear also contributed a number of airframes, designated <b>FG-3</b>, to the project. A single sub-variant <b>XF4U-3B</b> with minor modifications was also produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup> <b>XF4U-3B</b>, planned procurement for the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">FAA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>XF4U-4</b>: New engine and cowling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-4</b>: The last variant to see action during World War II, deliveries to the U.S. Navy of the F4U-4 began late in 1944, and this version fully equipped naval squadrons four months before the end of hostilities. It had the 2,100 hp (1,600 kW) dual-stage-supercharged -18W engine. When the cylinders were injected with the water/alcohol mixture, power was boosted to 2,450 hp (1,830 kW). The aircraft required an air scoop under the nose and the unarmored wing fuel tanks of 62 gal (234 l) capacities were removed for better maneuverability at the expense of maximum range. The propeller was changed to a four blade type. Maximum speed was increased to 448 miles per hour (721 km/h) and climb rate to over 4,500 ft/min (1,180 m/min) as opposed to the 2,900 ft/min (884 m/min) of the F4U-1A.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup> The "4-Hog" retained the original armament and had all the external load (i.e., drop tanks, bombs) capabilities of the F4U-1D. The windscreen was now flat bullet-resistant glass to avoid optical distortion, a change from the curved Plexiglas windscreens with the internal plate glass of the earlier Corsairs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Vought also tested the two F4U-4Xs (BuNos 49763 and 50301, prototypes for the new R2800) with fixed wing-tip tanks (the Navy showed no interest) and an <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aeroproducts_Aircraft_Propellers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aeroproducts Aircraft Propellers (page does not exist)">Aeroproducts</a> six-blade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraprop" title="Contraprop">contraprop</a> (not accepted for production).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="700" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg/220px-F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg/330px-F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg/440px-F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U-4_VF-1B_CVB-41_1947-48.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An F4U-4 of VF-1b on board USS Midway, 1947–1948.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>F4U-4B</b>: Designation for F4U-4s to be delivered to the British Fleet Air Arm, but were retained by the U.S. for its own use. The Fleet Air Arm received no F4U-4s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_p._148_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_p._148-122">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-4C</b>: 300 F4U-4s ordered with alternate gun armament of four 20 millimetres (0.79 in) <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">AN/M2 cannon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_p._148_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green_p._148-122">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-4E and F4U-4N</b>: Developed late in WWII, these night fighters featured radar radomes projecting from the right wingtip. The -4E was fitted with the APS-4 search radar, while the -4N was fitted with the APS-6 type. In addition, these aircraft were often refitted with four 20mm M2 cannons similar to the F4U-1C. Though these variants would not see combat during WWII, the night fighter variants would see great use during the Korean war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-4K</b>: Experimental drone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-4P</b>: As with the -1P, a rare photo reconnaissance variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qqnaio_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-qqnaio-115">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>XF4U-5</b>: New engine cowling, other extensive changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-4"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2338" data-file-width="3000" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg/220px-F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg/330px-F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg/440px-F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F4U-5N_VMFN-513_Wonsan_1950.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/VMA-513" title="VMA-513">VMF(N)-513</a> F4U-5N at <a href="/wiki/Wonsan" title="Wonsan">Wonsan</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, 1950.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>F4U-5</b>: A 1945 design modification of the F4U-4, first flown on 21 December 1945, was intended to increase the F4U-4 Corsair's overall performance and incorporate many Corsair pilots' suggestions. It featured a more powerful Pratt and Whitney R-2800-32(E) engine with a two-stage supercharger,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swanboroughp406_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-swanboroughp406-124">[117]</a></sup> rated at a maximum of 2,760 hp (2,060 kW). Other improvements included automatic blower controls, cowl flaps, intercooler doors and oil cooler for the engine, spring tabs for the elevators and rudder, a completely modernized cockpit, a completely retractable tail wheel, and heated cannon bays and pitot head. The cowling was lowered two degrees to help with forward visibility, but perhaps most striking as the first variant to feature all-metal wings (223 units produced).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup> Maximum speed was 408 knots (470 mph) and max rate of climb at sea level 4,850 feet per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-5N</b>: Radar equipped version (214 units produced)</p>
<p><b>F4U-5NL</b>: Winterized version (72 units produced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup> 29 modified from F4U-5Ns (101 total)). Fitted with rubber de-icing boots on the leading edge of the wings and tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>F4U-5P</b>: Long-range photo-reconnaissance version (30 units produced)</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1503" data-file-width="2090" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg/220px-AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg/330px-AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg/440px-AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AU-1_Corsair_in_flight_1952.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A factory-fresh AU-1, 1952.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>F4U-6</b>: Redesignated <b>AU-1</b>, this was a ground-attack version produced for the U.S. Marine Corps.</p>
<p><b>F4U-7</b> : AU-1 developed for the <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French Navy</a>.</p>
<p><b>FG-1E</b>: Goodyear FG-1 with radar equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-5"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>FG-1K</b>: Goodyear FG-1 as drone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-6"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>FG-3</b>: Turbosupercharger version converted from FG-1D.</p>
<p><b>FG-4</b>:Goodyear F4U-4, never delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_116-7"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>AU-1</b>:U.S. Marines attack variant with extra armor to protect the pilot and fuel tank, and the oil coolers relocated inboard to reduce vulnerability to ground fire. The supercharger was simplified as the design was intended for low-altitude operation. Extra racks were also fitted. Fully loaded for combat the AU-1 weighed 20% more than a fully loaded F4U-4, and was capable of carrying 8,200 lb of bombs. The AU-1 had a maximum speed of 238 miles per hour at 9,500 ft, when loaded with 4,600 lb of bombs and a 150-gallon drop-tank. When loaded with eight rockets and two 150-gallon drop-tanks, maximum speed was 298 mph at 19,700 ft. When not carrying external loads, maximum speed was 389 mph at 14,000 ft. First produced in 1952 and used in Korea, and retired in 1957. Re-designated from <b>F4U-6</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Super_Corsair_variants">Super Corsair variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Super Corsair variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Goodyear_F2G_Corsair" title="Goodyear F2G Corsair">Goodyear F2G Corsair</a></div>
<p>The <b>F2G-1</b> and <b>F2G-2</b> were significantly different aircraft, fitted with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_R-4360" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney R-4360">Pratt &amp; Whitney R-4360</a> Wasp Major 4-row 28-cylinder "corncob" radial engine and teardrop (bubble) canopy. The difference between the -1 and -2 variants was that the -1 featured a manual folding wing and 14 ft (4.3 m) propellers, while the F2G-2 aircraft had hydraulic operated folding wings, 13 ft (4.0 m) propellers and carrier arresting hooks for carrier use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup> As World War II was drawing to a close, development problems emerged that led to the abandonment of further work on the F2G series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup> While only 10 were built, several F2Gs went on to racing success after the war, winning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thompson_trophy" title="Thompson trophy">Thompson trophy</a> races in 1947 and 1949.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">

<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Argentine_Naval_Aviation" title="Argentine Naval Aviation">Argentine Naval Aviation</a> operated 26 F4U-5/5N/5NL Corsairs from 1956 to 1968 from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARA_Independencia_(V-1)" title="ARA Independencia (V-1)">ARA <i>Independencia</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1064" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg/23px-Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg/35px-Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg/46px-Flag_of_El_Salvador.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a></dt>
</dl>

</ul>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a></dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French Navy</a> operated 25 AU-1 and 94 F4U-7 from 1954 to 1964
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeronavale" title="Aeronavale">Aeronavale</a>
<ul>
<li><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=French_A%C3%A9ronavale_12.F_Flotilla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="French Aéronavale 12.F Flotilla (page does not exist)">French Aéronavale 12.F Flotilla</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algeria_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Algeria-98">[93]</a></sup></li>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_aircraft">Surviving aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Surviving aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_surviving_Vought_F4U_Corsairs" title="List of surviving Vought F4U Corsairs">List of surviving Vought F4U Corsairs</a></div>
<p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAA" title="FAA">FAA</a> there are 45 privately owned F4Us in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup> Also there is a F4U-4 Corsair with serial number 96995 owned by the <a href="/wiki/Flying_Bulls" title="Flying Bulls">Flying Bulls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Specifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="F4U-1A">F4U-1A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: F4U-1A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p style="font-size: 90%; margin:0; padding: .2em"><i>Data from</i> WWII Aircraft Performance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup> The Aviation History Online Museum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>General characteristics</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Crew:</b> 1 pilot</li>
<li><b>Length:</b> 33 ft 4 in (10.1 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">Wingspan</a>:</b> 41 ft 0 in (12.5 m)</li>
<li><b>Height:</b> 16 ft 1 in (4.90 m)</li>
<li><b>Wing area:</b> 314 ft<sup>2</sup> (29.17 m<sup>2</sup>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Manufacturer%27s_empty_weight" title="Manufacturer's empty weight">Empty weight</a>:</b> 8,982 lb (4,073 kg)</li>
<li><b>Loaded weight:</b> 11,432 lb (5,185 kg)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">Powerplant</a>:</b> 1 × <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_R-2800" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney R-2800">Pratt &amp; Whitney R-2800</a>-8 <a href="/wiki/Radial_engine" title="Radial engine">radial engine</a>, 2,000 hp (1,491 kW)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Performance</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/V_speeds#Regulatory_V-speeds" title="V speeds">Maximum speed</a>:</b> 417 mph (362 kn, 671 km/h)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Range_(aeronautics)" title="Range (aeronautics)">Range</a>:</b> 1,015 mi (882 nmi (1,633 km))</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ceiling_(aeronautics)" title="Ceiling (aeronautics)">Service ceiling</a>:</b> 36,900 ft (11,247 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rate_of_climb" title="Rate of climb">Rate of climb</a>:</b> 2,890 ft/min (15.2 m/s)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Armament</b></span><br/></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Guns:</b> 6 × 0.50 in (12.7 mm) <a href="/wiki/M2_Browning#AN.2FM2" title="M2 Browning">M2 Browning machine guns</a>, 400 rounds per gun</li>
<li><b>Bombs:</b> 2,000 pounds (910 kg)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="F4U-4">F4U-4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: F4U-4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2309" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG/300px-Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG/450px-Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG/600px-Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vought_AU-1_Corsair_Standard_Aircraft_Characterisics_1953.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
AU-1 Corsair Standard Aircraft Characteristics</div>
</div>
</div>
<p style="font-size: 90%; margin:0; padding: .2em"><i>Data from</i> F4U-4 Detail Specification;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F4U-4_Detail_Specification_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-F4U-4_Detail_Specification-142">[135]</a></sup> F4U-4 Airplane Characteristics and Performance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F4U-4_Airplane_Characteristics_and_Performance_.28March_1946.29_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-F4U-4_Airplane_Characteristics_and_Performance_.28March_1946.29-143">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>General characteristics</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Crew:</b> 1 pilot</li>
<li><b>Length:</b> 33 ft 8 in (10.2 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">Wingspan</a>:</b> 41 ft 0 in (12.5 m)</li>
<li><b>WS Folded:</b> 17 ft 0.5 in (5.2 m)</li>
<li><b>Height:</b> 14 ft 9 in (4.50 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Manufacturer%27s_empty_weight" title="Manufacturer's empty weight">Empty weight</a>:</b> 9,205 lb (4,174 kg)</li>
<li><b>Loaded weight:</b> 12,405 lb (5,626 kg)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">Powerplant</a>:</b> 1 × <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_R-2800" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney R-2800">Pratt &amp; Whitney R-2800</a>-18W <a href="/wiki/Radial_engine" title="Radial engine">radial engine</a>, 2,380 hp (1,775 kW)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Performance</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/V_speeds#Regulatory_V-speeds" title="V speeds">Maximum speed</a>:</b> 446 mph (717 km/h) at 26,200 ft (using emergency power)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Stall_(fluid_mechanics)#Stall_speed" title="Stall (fluid mechanics)">Stall speed</a>:</b> 89 mph (143 km/h) clean</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Range_(aeronautics)" title="Range (aeronautics)">Range</a>:</b> 1,005 mi (1617 km) on internal fuel</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combat_radius" title="Combat radius">Combat radius</a>:</b> 285 nmi (328 mi, 527 km) with one external 150gal tank</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ceiling_(aeronautics)" title="Ceiling (aeronautics)">Service ceiling</a>:</b> 41,500ft (12,649 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rate_of_climb" title="Rate of climb">Rate of climb</a>:</b> 4,360ft/min (22.1 m/s) at sea-level (using emergency power)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Armament</b></span><br/></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Guns:</b><br/>
<ul>
<li>6 × 0.50 in (12.7 mm) <a href="/wiki/M2_Browning#AN.2FM2" title="M2 Browning">M2 Browning machine guns</a>, 400 rounds per gun <i>or</i></li>
<li>4 × 0.79 in (20 mm) <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404#United_States" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">AN/M3 cannon</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b>Rockets:</b> 8 × 5 in (12.7 cm) high velocity aircraft rockets <i>and/or</i></li>
<li><b>Bombs:</b> 4,000 pounds (1,800 kg)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_appearances_in_media">Notable appearances in media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Notable appearances in media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">

<td class="mbox-image">

</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Flying_Leathernecks" title="Flying Leathernecks">Flying Leathernecks</a></i> (1951) starring <a href="/wiki/John_Wayne" title="John Wayne">John Wayne</a>, was about a Marine Corps squadron flying Corsairs while developing close-support tactics.</li>
<li>The exploits of Marine Corps squadron <a href="/wiki/VMA-214" title="VMA-214">VMF-214</a>, which flew the Corsair in the Pacific during the war, were depicted in the popular 1976 made-for-television movie <i>Baa Baa Black Sheep</i> (also released as <i>Flying Misfits</i>) and the follow-up <a href="/wiki/Television_program" title="Television program">television series</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Baa_Baa_Black_Sheep_(TV_series)" title="Baa Baa Black Sheep (TV series)">Baa Baa Black Sheep</a></i>, also called <i>Black Sheep Squadron</i>, which aired from 1976 to 1978. The television series featured six genuine flying Corsairs, but the storylines were fictional. See also <a href="/wiki/Pappy_Boyington" title="Pappy Boyington">Pappy Boyington</a>.</li>
<li>The Corsair plays a prominent role in W.E.B. Griffin's book series, <i>The Corps</i> (1986–present).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a> served as a flight instructor training young Marines to fly Corsairs while away from major league baseball during his years of military service in World War II.</li>
<li>The Disney <i><a href="/wiki/Cars_Toons" title="Cars Toons">Cars Toons</a></i> short "Air Mater" (2013) and the spin-off movies <i><a href="/wiki/Planes_(film)" title="Planes (film)">Planes</a></i> (2013) and <i><a href="/wiki/Planes:_Fire_%26_Rescue" title="Planes: Fire &amp; Rescue">Planes: Fire &amp; Rescue</a></i> (2014), features a F4U Corsair named Skipper Riley. Skipper is voiced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stacey_Keach" title="Stacey Keach">Stacey Keach</a>.</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vought_F4U_Corsair&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A landing technique using a curving approach that kept the LSO (landing signal officer) in view while coming aboard was developed by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> and was adopted by the U.S. Navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">F2A Buffalos and F4F Wildcats used similar glazed panels. Prior to the F4U-4, Corsair cockpits did not have a complete floor.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although P/O <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mynarski" title="Andrew Mynarski">Andrew Mynarski</a>'s Victoria Cross was actually awarded in 1946, it commemorated an action in 1944.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although these are often call <b>F4U-1A</b>s, apparently this was not an official wartime designation but was one applied postwar to indicate that there were production line modifications. The same comment applies to the <b>-1B</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_1984.2C_p._27._35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1984.2C_p._27.-35">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A photograph exists of a F4U-1 being examined on the ground by Japanese airmen.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A later version of this canopy incorporated a small rear view mirror in a transparent blister.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although F4Us operated by the Marines were seldom seen with folded wings it did not mean that this facility was deactivated; the only version of the Corsair built without folding wings were some of those manufactured by Goodyear.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
